title: "Do college students use Linux?"
date: 2007-09-10

### Post by SZF2001 on 2007-09-10
In this day and age, a lot of people are going to end up on one computer or another, I suppose. Rather it be a laptop or desktop, kids need to study.

Here is my question - are there a bunch of college kids running Linux, or is the general average Joe still going to be using Windows? No offense to them, of course, they are just sticking to what they know.

I was just curious about the subject - it would be neat to meet other people using Ubuntu or Mandrake, maybe Fedora, you know. I bet there are some classes I could take, too...

---

### Post by reckless2k2 on 2007-09-10
there was a post recently out about this. it seems only IT related schools are using and or supporting linux. most schools across the country are not supporting it and therefore the students are not using it.

### Post by maniacmusician on 2007-09-10
At my college (Hampshire College in Amherst, MA), the school recommends Macs over everything else, so that's what's most prevalent here. Some people still use Windows, and I've actually met more than 5 which use Linux (this is a very small school, so that was surprising). Our IT department actually does support Linux for people that want to use it, and I hear about people wanting to try it all the time, so I think it's getting good exposure on college campuses.

---

### Post by ThinkBuntu on 2007-09-10
At my University nearly everyone runs Mac or Windows, and people are very tech illiterate, since it's a Liberal Arts school and not an Ivy League one. I have yet to encounter a Linux user or hear anyone mention Linux. If I weren't a web designer, I would have sold my Macbook and would be running Zenwalk on my old ThinkPad...

The college I was going to go to only supported Windows and Mac OS X. I would have used Linux anyway. But I ended up not going there.:p

### Post by JNowka on 2007-09-10
I am a college student. I have helped to open the doors to Linux in my community college. They are about the implement their first linux server, and are going to start introducing students to linux over a two week period in a software certification class. Not only that but I am currently pushing for a full fledged linux class, and though the ground ahead is shaky, it also looks promising.
 
So my advice to other college students is to be vocal to the staff and faculty of your college. They are likly to listen when their students request new material.

### Post by reckless2k2 on 2007-09-10
if the college supports networking with a mac, you can more than likely do linux then but it'll be unsupported. "choice" is typically restricted because of the support factor mostly. the IT world is dominated by microsoft certifications. the schools need supported software and look to what the industry points to. all in all it leads back to a microsoft dominated world in general. i'm sure most not just about everything you might need could be done with linux but you'll get no support on it. it'll be a good learning experience though bugging around with it. i had something similar in a work environment. the only tough thing was VPN from home to the workplace.

### Post by argie on 2007-09-10
I use Linux and I'm in college. What I've noticed about people here is lots use it in college because it's required but nearly no one uses it on the desktop. However, the better the college, the higher the chance that someone will be using Linux or a Mac.

In fact, there's this really good place for Math here called CMI, and AFAIK, there is not one windows computer on the whole campus.

### Post by WarholsGhost on 2007-09-10

There is a LUG on my campus but it is very inactive on a campus wide level. My school seems to use more macs in the art department  but windows is everywhere else, linux is only found on the 12 computers mentioned above.

### Post by p_quarles on 2007-09-10
I'm a grad student, and live off-campus, so I don't have a lot of experience connecting with my university's (U of Chicago) network, other than for e-mail and library services. 

That said, they the IT department does a good job of being flexible about OSes. The main servers all run *nix (with e-mail via SSH and Pine, or via IMAP), they actively distribute Firefox/Thunderbird as part of the "connectivity package", and they have Cisco's VPN client available for Windows, Mac and Linux. They don't offer tech support for Linux users, but they do a great job of making the entire network available to them.

In *stark* contrast is another local university at which I took a temp job earlier this year. The web servers were running on *nix, but that was about it. Staff and faculty had the option of using Mac desktops, but the Exchange server prevented them from getting much connectivity (they had to use the web interface to communicate with the server). 

Just as I was wrapping up my assignment, they started porting all multimedia materials (lecture recordings, etc.) onto a localized iTunes store. In other words, materials that had previously been available in widely accessible formats could now only be downloaded using the iTunes client. I still can't figure out why they would do this.

---

### Post by matt_man22 on 2007-09-10

### Post by macogw on 2007-09-10
I'd say probably 20-25% of kids here use Macs, and most of the rest use Windows.  About 1/4 of kids in my CS classes raised their hands for Linux when the professor asked what OS we use, but outside CS, there are few Linux users here.  One is a girl who was in my Japanese class.  Her boyfriend saw me using Ubuntu and then installed it on both his computer and hers, so she's still using it.  

The Physics department uses CentOS.  The Computer Science department has a lot of XP/Fedora Core 4 dual-boots (I think they upgraded to Fedora 7 on some of them recently), a few XP/Fedora dual boots that are hooked up to Macs with KVMs, and at least one Mac lab.

We're told that it is "impossible" to get wireless here with Linux or BSD.  I know it's BS.  I made an instruction sheet and gave 10 copies to Student Technology Services.  Apparently the guy I handed them to gave them to the guy in charge of STS, and he was impressed with it (neither of them knew how to do it even though both use Linux), so I have a job interview Wednesday morning.

### Post by gpilkay on 2007-09-10
I use a lot of Open-Source stuff, including Linux.  Reason being, though, I have to deal with a LOT of different file formats and it's actually easier for things like OpenOffice and GIMP to handle then the rather sparse cross-compatibility platforms you can get under Windows.  I also use Linux extensively on the on-line classes as it's much less crash-prone when dealing with the campus servers.

Given that they SELL OpenSuse in the bookstore at UNCP and give classes ON Linux, I'm not surprised it has its uses here.

### Post by DarkOx on 2007-09-10
I'm pretty much the only student I've seen using Linux as their personal desktop. However, both our Computer Science and Engineering computer labs have some version of linux running on them. But I haven't had much experience with them since that's not my program.

For my major, business, no one cares what OS you run just what office suite. Obviously, Microsoft Office is what's taught and what's expected, seeing as how it's the tool of choice for the vast majority of businesses. Open Office can more or less substitute for Word, but Calc doesn't quite match up with the features we're being exposed to in Excel. But, if you wanted to run Linux, and could either virtualize or use Crossover Office or whatever, it'd certainly be accepted.

### Post by sloggerkhan on 2007-09-10
I use linux only on my computer at the University of Arizona.
IT is fairly decentralized here, so different groupsof people have different setups. The CS department has mostly windows with some Fedora desktops and also at least one *nix server. Engineering has a number of *nix servers and different labs have mac and windows. So far as the CS department goes, I don't get the sense that they have a major open source bent, but I don't think they are opposed to it or anything.

I can't think of a university service that I can't access with linux. (Thought there are some that will only work with Firefox/Explorer/Safari/Opera browsers, so no niche browsers for some stuff.) I guess there is some experimental support of the iTunes music store for lecture podcasts, but I've never had a class with that.

I know at least 4 or 5 other Ubuntu/Linux users. Different majors, some math, some cs, some engineering. In my CS classes so far, it seems like a few people use linux and a bit more dabble with it.

Our campus wide secure wireless network doesn't work very well for me, but the problems with it aren't linux related... a lot of people on mac and windows have issues, too.
OSCR, the people that offer computer support have a forum with a linux section and make a go at offering support. [http://forum.oscr.arizona.edu/forumdisplay.php?f=39](http://forum.oscr.arizona.edu/forumdisplay.php?f=39) Supposedly they have some linux people who will also try to help if you come in.

Most of the users I meet who don't use linux either have antagonistic stereotypes about it, or are afraid to try it. That said, there seems to be an increasing use of open source software on windows and mac.

---

### Post by Tomosaur on 2007-09-10
> **SZF2001 said:**
> In this day and age, a lot of people are going to end up on one computer or another, I suppose. Rather it be a laptop or desktop, kids need to study.

Here is my question - are there a bunch of college kids running Linux, or is the general average Joe still going to be using Windows? No offense to them, of course, they are just sticking to what they know.

I was just curious about the subject - it would be neat to meet other people using Ubuntu or Mandrake, maybe Fedora, you know. I bet there are some classes I could take, too...

Well, I'm in University, I'm taking a Software Development course. While the instructors mostly do everything on Windows - I personally use Linux for all of my work - since we don't do any Windows specific stuff. It is annoying, however, when lecturers send out course notes in MS .doc format - I can open them, but they should use an open format as a matter of principle.

The 'ordinary' computers in the libraries all run Windows. In the computer labs (which is where I go if I need to use a computer while I'm actually at the university), you log in through Windows, then you can connect to a Linux session using something called 'Hummingbird'. There are various different distros in use, but they're all set up to have the same software and such-like. It's fairly random which distro you get logged into. The most common to get is Fedora, but I've logged into Knoppix a few times. I don't THINK Ubuntu is one of the available distros, but I could be wrong.

The sys admins seem to be fairly schizophrenic regarding Linux / Windows. All of the machines default to Windows, but they seem to reccommend that you use Linux for everything.

Ironically, using Linux via a remote session is still faster than using Windows on that machine. First thing I do when I log on (if I'm going to be there for more than 5 minutes) is just sign in to Linux - everything loads so much faster. Loading anything on Windows seems to take forever - I can log in to Linux and fire up everything I need in the time it takes for Internet Explorer to load on Windows :/

---

### Post by isaacj87 on 2007-09-10
I'm a sophomore in college and I switched over from XP to Ubuntu. The switch was easy for me because I had no particular allegiance to any OS (even though I was pretty comfortable with XP at the time). My girlfriend's Dad had put Ubuntu on her laptop and she suggested I try it. I did...**and I was hooked**! I was a computer science major (recently turned biology major) but my major nor the classes I was taking had any influence on my decision to move to Linux. I've been a software and tech junkie every since I could remember and could be considered myself a Windows fanboy (seeing how I've used windows since 3.0). So, I just wanted to see how good Ubuntu/Linux really was and if it was more secure, more stable and more beautiful than XP. 

**It is.** :)

### Post by Atreus12 on 2007-09-10
I am working on my senior design project in Mechanical Engineering, and all three members of group use linux. 2 Ubuntu users, and one Debian user.

Of course, that might be a bit misleading. Most kids do not use linux. Myself and another member of the group have used Linux for a while, the third started  using Ubuntu after his hard drive failed.

Our IT department unofficially supports Linux. Meaning that if you go in and ask, they will help you but officially they do not support linux.

Edit: Engineering server is a *nix server, so it makes ssh-ing convenient.

### Post by Skye on 2007-09-14
Here at RIT (Rochester Institute of Technology) we have a pretty diverse computing network.  I've seen tons of people running macs, as well as tons of people running vista and XP.  There's more than a few people running some form of linux in my dorm, and they aren't even all CS majors- I'm in engineering, and I run linux.

There's no reason not to run linux here, as far as I can tell.  All of the software we use in courses are on lab computers, and cost 6 or 7 thousand dollars a pop anyway (though they are windows-only programs, AFAIK.)  Campus e-mail is availible online through a web access page, through an exchange server, or through a pop server, so that's effectively platform independent.  Most of our classwork/assignments/grades are posted online using something called "MyCourses" which works on anything that can run IE/Firefox.

Of course, this is a pretty tech-oriented college, so the average knowledge about computers is probably higher here than it is at, say, a liberal arts college.

### Post by mister_doctor on 2007-09-14
This caught my eye so I thought I'd drop in my story. There's a bit of background so please bear with, I'll try to keep it low on rant-fat.

My school is called Durham College, in Oshawa Ontario. It's become a pretty high-tech campus over the past couple of years, with the introduction of the provinces newest university, University of Ontario Institute of Technology (stupid name I know).

Anyway, I'm a 3rd year in a program called Computer Systems Technology. Part of our tuition includes a lease on a laptop (IBM R60s this year) containing all of the software we need for the program. Unfortunately, its a Windows image and not a very good one. It includes all of IBM's bloatware, and a pig of an AntiVirus (F-Secure). One of the guys took it upon himself to make his own image, with all the software we need and none of the crap we don't. I, on the otherhand, had other ideas.

I wanted to see if it was possible to run a linux install that was just as functional as the Windows image. So far I've been doing well, and Ubuntu has been the most laptop-friendly image so far. I still have some work to do, so for now I have to dual-boot with a barebones XP install.

---

### Post by NiklasV on 2007-09-14
I'm a CS student at the Royal Institute of Technology in Stockholm. The mainstay of our desktops are Solaris terminals, with a few Windows and Mac computers. I think most other departments are mostly Windows based though, but I know for a fact that some have Linux desktops.
I think the servers are mostly Solaris, not sure though.

So far, (I'm in my second year) all the software we have used has been cross-platform. BlueJ and Eclipse for Java programming, (Eclipse was recommended in the introductory CS course in the first year, though not required), Matlab for numerical analysis and Hugs for Haskell programming. Written assignments that are turned in in electronic form are generally required to be in PDF, and we're encouraged, though not required, to write it in LaTeX. (Word processors don't even come close to the power of LaTeX, and the output always looks much better with LaTeX)

I would estimate about a third to half of CS students are running Linux on their laptops. Some that don't still run Linux on their desktop and/or server though.

### Post by krazikamikaze on 2007-09-15
I do EECS at Berkeley and as you might expect there's a lot of OSS here including linux (well unix actually, but close enough).

We have a windows network and a Solaris network. Almost every EE/CS class gives you an account on one of the networks. CS classes are almost always unix accounts and EE classes are usually windows accounts with some exceptions.

At the very least we use our unix accounts to submit assignments and check our grades. In the introductory CS class they teach us how to use emacs (though I'm a vi person myself) and the basics of navigating the command line. Most classes use command line programs like gcc, make, scheme, ns, LaTeX, etc. or GUIs that run in unix like eclipse or ethereal. There are usually windows equivalents of these programs or they can be run under cygwin but it's not recommended because when stuff is graded it's always run on the solaris machines.

Almost all of the [computer labs]("http://inst.eecs.berkeley.edu/~inst/iesglabs.html") in our CS building (Soda) are Solaris machines or we can always SSH into the network.

I'm running Ubuntu exclusively now, but I've run Windows while here in the past and I find it significantly easier to do the work in the classes when I'm running linux at home.

### Post by stim on 2007-10-04
I'm a senior a the  University of Delaware , majoring in Computer Science.
I have a Dell xps that boots ubuntu and vista (xp blue screens on install....) , I also have a notebook that books ubuntu and xp.
All of my hardware on both systems is fully functional with Feisty.
Even the dreaded bcm43xx driver worked right away with fw-cutter.  :)
If you want fancy-pants graphical effects like a mac, get beryl. Runs a 3d desktop and all sorts of cool effects, even on my 1.4ghz\1gig laptop, with 32meg integrated video. :shock:
I also have my desktop running an ssh daemon so that I can use it as a server for group work and remote programming.

I am no genius, but I have definitely learned alot from tinkering, and have been able to apply of what I learn in class to what I want to do at home.

Except for the fact that Day of Defeat: Source , Red Orchestra and Company of Heroes only run in windows....Id only use linux.  
note:my HP deskjet 6122 printer works with linux but not with vista!! :lolflag:

Basically, I love it.

### Post by WedgeHG on 2007-10-26
I go to Cal Poly Pomona and have Ubuntu on my laptop. My desktop dual-boots XP and Fiesty. I am a CIS major and don't know of a single other student who uses linux regularly. Windows is predominant among students but seeing a Mac laptop or 2 is fairly common.

I'm also a student assistant in the I&IT Systems department (that department is basically in charge of the network infrastructure). Despite the fact that we don't officially support linux, all but one of the staff members of the I&IT systems department use linux, though they prefer Gentoo. The other student assistants I work with use Windows.

I'd say that a majority of the school's servers are Solaris with Windows coming in a close second. Some Gentoo servers as well.  For the record we have an Exchange server and I didn't have any trouble getting Thunderbird to work with it.

I still do need windows from time to time for certain things. Had to do some projects with an online version of Quickbooks that only works on IE, some Excel plugin that didn't have an Open Office equivalent, and my database class is using SQL Server 2005. Also have had some minor difficulties with our Blackboard system, namely submitting files but I've found a workaround to that (PM me if you have this issue). 

So the only thing that i haven't been able to get working with Ubuntu is the LEAP authenticated wireless. This is in spite of reading the how-to's on this forum and the fact that I share a room with the IIT people who run the thing (I sorta am one of them)!  :lolflag:

### Post by SomeGuyDude on 2007-11-21
Why would Apple want someone to go to Linux? They make no extra money if I use iTunes on Windows or Ubuntu.

However, it would wreck their own efforts if they put iTunes on Linux. Remember Apple's whole thing is "get away from PC's", meaning Windows. It's entirely in their best interest to keep it a "two party race" and make people think the only legitimate options are Windows and OSX, and that if you're smart you'll pick up OSX.

If they put their software on Linux, they'd be supporting a way to get away from Windows that gives them (Apple) zero money. Remember, the only way to get OSX is to buy a Mac. You can't buy it and put it on your Dell Latitude. So what would Apple gain by encouraging people to pick up a free OS that doesn't require a new machine?

---

Ubuntu also wasn't as polished back then, so it made things like audio and video codecs and flash much more annoying to setup. :p

### Post by gnp421 on 2007-11-21
I'm in my final year of college, I'm getting a degree in Electronics Engineering with a concentration in Computer/Digital Systems. I also just got hired to maintin the Solaris machines at my campus. The campus is Mostly windows based and almost everyone uses it. There are the occassional linux users but mostly windows. ### Post by Zyphrexi on 2007-11-21
I'm in college. I use linux. Mostly self taught. Actually completely self taught.

It's annoying but there isn't much linux activity at my school. While there are a couple classes, I can't think of them as useful in any way, since they teach using red hat, which I abhor. The general access computer labs all run windows, I always spend about five minutes hunting for a terminal before realizing I'm using windows. I have on occasion booted damnsmall from a usb drive, but apparently I'm not supposed to do that either. Really annoying when you have a paper due and the only access you have cannot convert filetypes. 

I've done papers in abiword, openoffice, imported sketches via Kseg or Dr. Geo (both excellent programs for geometry students). For math, it usually involves a lot of hunting and pecking for symbols, since I really have  no desire to learn LaTeX. For tables and charts, I've used gnumeric, which was difficult to find exactly what the professor wanted in the exact format he wanted it, but I managed.

For the student, linux and free software is superior when you do not have thousands of dollars with which to purchase the annoying software required by the collegiate powers that be. (and have no desire to pirate such software, even though that is what most college students do)

However, teachers or professors who do not accept open standards are probably the greatest difficulty in using linux and free software in an educational institution, since a great amount of file conversion is necessary. I would like to see classes not simply teaching use of linux, but the use of programs within linux, since that would really have been an aid to me in those times where time was running out.

---

### Post by ghandi69_ on 2007-12-13
I am a engineering student at the university of Iowa, and I use linux.

Also, we have 3 computer labs in the engineering building, and in each of them, half of the computers(roughly 20) have Linux (OpenSuse) installed on them.  Other things of note.

All the windows computers have 19 inch 4:3 flat panels.
All the linux machines have 24 inch wide screen flat panels.

The linux machines, as mentioned, come with Open Suse, but they also have almost every available free program known to man installed on there.

I'm talking every web browser (konq, opera, seamonkey, firefox etc), every media player, every little 2D game.  

They also have Matlab and other engineering type software on them like Eclipse, KDevelop, Anjuta, and Cadence.

Every single Desktop Manager or WIndow manager that I know of have been installed on these machines, and you can select which one you want to use on loging.

Gnome, KDE, XFCE, fluxbox, openbox, blackbox, icewm etc...

### Post by DouglasAWh on 2008-01-13
I'll admit to not ready through all these posts, just the beginning and end, but...

I'm a Co-Founder in the Carolina Open Source Initiative, a student group and UNC-Chapel Hill.  We've helped get a staff group going too, call the Open Source Software Working Group.  We are NOT a LUG.  The school has one of those...at least in the form of a listserv.  Being that ibiblio.org is hosted here (was metalab, was sunsite), open source is a big deal to a least some of us on the campus.  We've got 121 members on our facebook group [http://unc.facebook.com/group.php?gid=2232238246](http://unc.facebook.com/group.php?gid=2232238246) and our two co-presidents aren't even on facebook.  Again, we are not a LUG, but we are trying expand Linux on campus.

If anybody would be interested in speaking at our campus, please let me know.  If COSI cannot do it and you're worth bringing to campus, ibiblio might be able to do it.

Here's our website: [http://www.ibiblio.org/cosi/](http://www.ibiblio.org/cosi/)

### Post by jcwmoore on 2008-01-13
I am a Student at Missouri State University (USA) and on the main campus most people use windows XP.  In the physics department they use a linux OS for use with Astronomy software but other than that most departments do not endorse it.  Now with that said the new User Support Coordinator has done a lot of good things, as far a promoting FOSS.  In the three main computer labs every computer has OpenOffice and Gimp.  The resource CD's (that have a University Dial up program) has OO.o, GIMP and other open source programs on it.  In one of the labs there are even linux VM's that students can use.  Now with all that linux users like myself are still few and very far between, Even the in Computer Science department...:(

### Post by Arkenzor on 2008-02-04
Many of my fellow Computer Science students are still terribly reluctant to go Tux after our first semester, it's a surprise to me but I think most of them will end un making the switch.
Well, I did see a certain introductory course on databases with M$ Access at the center of its subject matter (somehow made me want to cry), but on the whole our CS department is clearly Linux based.


Seems non-IT students stick to the FUD thinking though. They're certainly pretty active in defending their OS in the Great Battle of Commercial Systems That Rip You Off (Mine Doesn't But Yours Does), but show no interest in Linux at all even though they'd be able to get more support for it than in any other context. The school (except for the IT department) is basically promoting Mac because it makes them feel so special compared to the mass of windows users.

That's too bad though, because college really is a great environment for trying and learning Linux. Let me be proof, I upgraded from interested noob to almost full-time user in less than one week thanks to all the people around who helped me.


Also, the fact that the whole university's IT stuff is handled by the CS department leads to the funny situation that all students are told to go Mac/Windows by their professors, yet most network services can be used much more easily from Linux. Call it a sweet revenge...

### Post by manimal347 on 2008-02-04
I'm at UMass Amherst, and while our sole Linux computer room is never open and OITUNIX (it ran on Solaris) got canned and our LUG is dormant, help desk support is available for all (current) flavors of Ubuntu, as well as for CentOS, Redhat, and Fedora. You can probably get help for other distributions provided they're similar/derivatives, or you're asking a general question like "how do I configure Thunderbird to connect to the mail server?"

Any computer that runs an OS with DHCP support and has an Ethernet board with an RJ45 connector or RJ45 adapter can connect to the campus Internet. Yes, that even includes an MS-DOS 3.3 machine with 640K ram, and a 5 1/4" boot drive. Yes, it gets DHCP support once you load a net stack into memory. With OITUNIX gone, though, such minimal setups are less useful and more cumbersome. I decommissioned my relic, but I suppose I could get on Lynx through a freenet and NCSA telenet, and read my Umai and Gmaill with a locally installed copy of Pine.

I'll give them a B for consideration, as it wouldn't kill them to offer a few Ubuntu boxes in the Library and have copies of OpenOffice available on the Windows boxes. Seriously, when the library gets really crunched for machines, people would probably even settle for a 486 box; anything with a Web browser and word processors begins to look good. I'm sure the Ubuntu machines would not go unused in periods of high demand.

How many people run Linux? Very few, and most are comp-sci majors. As a humanities major, I'm in the great minority. Many of these people who run Linux dualboot it, but I see that as defeating the purpose of keeping a free system.

/Debian user, for what it's worth
//I also use Ubuntu Server
///We're really all part of the same family, just Ubuntu users are often more "green," and can avoid the command line most of the time if they so wish.

### Post by DouglasAWh on 2008-02-05
We started in March 2007 officially, though February 2007 is when we started to get the ball rolling.  We're not a chapter of OSI.  We based our name on CCI - Carolina Computing Initiative.  I sent a couple e-mails to the webmaster at OSI and the Tiemann, but never got a response.  I sat beside Tiemann at a FOSS roundtable back in October, and he didn't say anything about us using the name, so I think we're good.

What's the URL for your chapter of OSI?  Who's in charge?  I was a founder of COSI, but have stepped down as president because I am graduating in May.  Jon Bersuder and [Cristobal Palmer]("http://claimid.com/cristobalpalmer") are the current presidents.

---

### Post by macogw on 2008-02-13
> **kenono said:**

You can use Virtualbox in "seamless" mode.  It's a virtual machine.  You run Windows in it, but then the programs you're running can sit right on the Linux desktop instead of being inside a little box.  [Codeweavers](http://codeweavers.com) makes a product called Crossover Office which is like Wine but a bit "souped up" to make it work well for Microsoft Office.

### Post by conphara on 2008-02-15
When I started in college back in the bachelor days, I used Win2000 on an old Toshiba notebook. Served me well. We used MS Office for turning in assignments via e-mails that had to be *.doc, *.xls AND Times New Roman by default. Every teacher I have ever met has always referred spreadsheets to Excel, never used the word spreadsheet: "if you open up Excel and......" That's the same as calling a vacuum cleaner a Hoover instead of a vacuum cleaner. 
I started with OpenSUSE because of Compiz. Later I started using Ubuntu and used that intensively as a replacement for everything Windows related. Used OO for taking notes in all my classes and turning in rapports in doc/xls as expected. Impress for all my presentations. Nobody never noticed any differences related to MS Office. Dia for Visio-like flowchart diagrams.
Evolution's calender and Tomboy notes so I would never forget any assignments or important dates. Great *FREE* tools for any student. Highly recommendable.

I actually did my thesis and a lot of my exams using OO (and other stuff), so it CAN be done, regardless of what other people tell you. 
Using open source as a student is mainly about the APPS, not the OS. But using Linux or BSD gives you a higher level of stability and security. Really bad getting a virus or sudden shutdowns before or during an exam if you don't do backups. With open source I personally never had to worry about that, unlike my earlier experiences with Windows and experiences from some of my friends.
Using wireless or wired at school (WEP/WPA); no problem.
Also some people say, you can't work in groups if one group member is using OO or a different OS (=Stupid people). Not true - at least not in my case. Of course I never used OO Base or any VBA related. Some Excel files won't work well in Calc (mainly because of VBA) but looking back I never had any problems receiving files from teachers and classmates or giving back. One teacher gave us a homemade Stats tool made from Excel and VBA, but that was my first year when I used Win so I dont know if it works in OO or not. 

The negative responces on using open source was from one of my Finance professors about the translation for a Calc forumla. "Not correctly translated and rather misleading", which I can't blame him. That goes for a number of them, but of course the calculations are correct though.

I live in Denmark and in my all my years of college, I have never met anyone using open source. Of course, Denmark is a very Microsoft-loving country, even though more and more are starting buying rotten apples.

Looking back I really loved the reactions from classmates or professors when doing a presentation on a big screen and swithing to a different app and used the cube (with an animated skydome): What was that you just did right there??????? 
Or using Magic Lamp to minimize apps, Expose, Showing Desktop or Dashboard with Widgets, and looking over at the mac people and saying to myself, "You had to pay for your eyecandy, I didn't."

On a serious note. Most people will experience that getting a job after college will result in using Windows and MS Office. Using Windows (XP,Vista) and for instance Ubuntu isn't really that different. The same goes for Firefox vs IE and MSO vs OO. 
In my line of work, people are expecting you to be more than familiar with Word and Excel. But I'm actually helping people using Excel and Powerpoint at the office. 
The way that I see it, open source is only making us better. 
And remember the more mac users this society is getting, the more people you'll notice saying something like, "I'm not used to doing it like this (=using Windows), this works so much better at home using my mac." True story!

---

### Post by macogw on 2008-02-15
> **conphara said:**
> 
On a serious note. Most people will experience that getting a job after college will result in using Windows and MS Office. Using Windows (XP,Vista) and for instance Ubuntu isn't really that different. The same goes for Firefox vs IE and MSO vs OO. 
In my line of work, people are expecting you to be more than familiar with Word and Excel. But I'm actually helping people using Excel and Powerpoint at the office. 
In school when you learn Word, Excel, and Powerpoint, you are taught to memorize a series of clicks.  When you have used and are comfortable with Word, Excel, Powerpoint, Writer, Calc, and Impress, you are no longer using a memorized series of clicks that fails if you forget one little part and is probably a little different depending on which you're using.  Instead, you are finding the next logical step based on what's available.

When I was a kid, I had Microsoft Works word processor at home and Microsoft Word at school, then I got Corel Word Perfect at home.  Eventually I got Microsoft Word, but it doesn't much matter.  Word processing is word processing.  They're all essentially the same, and the sooner school technology teachers realize that memorizing is bad and learning to adapt to different environments is good, the better.

### Post by macogw on 2008-02-28
In the school Starbucks today, there were 2 Linux laptops, 6 laptops that probably ran OSX, and 7 that probably ran Windows.  Windows had less than 1/2.  Despite this, the housing application for next year's dorms can only be completed using Internet Explorer.  The school knew when they bought it that it was IE-only.  They also know we have a ton of Mac users and a few Linux users.  They're idiots. 

It was in our school paper ([link](http://media.www.gwhatchet.com/media/storage/paper332/news/2008/02/25/News/Mac-Users.Unable.To.Use.Ihousing-3231816.shtml?reffeature=popuarstoriestab)).  I wrote a letter to the editor:
> 
**The Administrators Seem to be Clueless**

This thing where they block all non-Windows (please note that Mac OS is not the only non-Windows OS, there are plenty of Linux and FreeBSD users on campus, we just never need to take our stuff in for repair at STS) computers from accessing iHousing is just the most recent in a long list of events that shows the administration is clueless about everything that doesn't pertain to politics, international affairs, and general bureaucracy.  

Ever had to have your locks replaced because you lost your key?  The school claims it is for security purposes.  Fine.  If that's that case, why does it take them two or three months to finally get around to it?  That's not security!  It should take, at most, a week.

Ever had your internet connection terminated because you were supposedly on a botnet?  Running Linux means I didn't end up on a botnet, but why did it take ISS two full weeks to order that STS disconnect my internet when they saw malware download?  Two full weeks?!  If I had been running Windows, half the Windows users on the network would have been infected by that point!  That response time should have been under 24 hours.

It is obvious that the school only takes these "security measures" so they can put it as a little bullet on a slide about how much they protect the students.  They certainly aren't doing it to produce any sort of real security.
We'll see if it gets published.

### Post by jordanmthomas on 2008-02-28
> **macogw said:**
> 
We'll see if it gets published.

I hope it does.  I am considering writing a similar letter to my history professor.  After the semester started she decided to make us use a product called "Respondus Lockdown Browser" to take weekly tests.  As you may expect, it doesn't support Linux and doesn't work in wine.  Here's a few "features" the program has.  
[LIST]
[*]Shut down all other applications
[*]Disable Alt-Tab
[*]Disable Ctrl-Alt-Delete
[*]Disable shutting down your computer
[*]In the event it crashes, these functions aren't restored
[*]Will not let you close it until you are done with a test
[/LIST]

Now, while I am against this kind of software philosophically, I would still use it *had she informed us we'd have to use it at the beginning of the semester* or put it in her syllabus.  The whole ordeal has really annoyed me.  My solution was to install Windows in a VM and install this browser there.  This has the side-effect of effectively eliminating the point of the browser all together since I can just resize the VM's window (I don't, but I very easily could).  

Anyway, before I wrote the letter I wanted to make sure I wasn't crazy and that some fellow Linux users agree it's ridiculous to force this kind of software on people.

### Post by ack389 on 2008-05-11
I'm a computer science student at The University of Iowa and yes it is supported, especially in engineering, math, and computer departments.  The main computer scince lab is all linux except two windows server editions at the front.

### Post by Phil Airtime on 2008-05-11
I've had dealings with a third-rate university in the English Midlands where Linux is unheard-of, even in the computing department. Students learn to code in (excuse me if I get a bit of terminology wrong here) ASP.NET and Visual Basic! They have the occasional Mac dotted around for student use, but the vast majority of computers are Windows.

I think that if you're not doing courses which require specialist software (Final Cut for TV production, Quark for newspaper layout etc) then it's rather disingenuous for a university to recommend students purchase a Mac or Windows computer for themselves. Education should be universal and not based on whether you can afford the latest fancy computer or not; Linux, for all its faults, is the single most affordable computer system, can be run on almost any dodgy old second-hand box and is more than capable of typing up essays, internet research, time management, email, Facebook, shameless plagiarism and whatever else students generally do. That's part of the reason I like it--no cost to me, no dodgy pirate software needed. Outside of student-land, I'm aware of more than one non-profit workplace that's set up its computers with Ubuntu.

---

### Post by xtang on 2008-05-11

So far all my course work has been done on linux, fedora 7 in the first semester, linux mint in the second.

### Post by lars-5 on 2008-05-11
Just graduated from college, and only know at most 3 other people that use linux, all in computer science - there's probably more.  students are split mac [liberal arts] and windows [sciences and engineering], while the computer labs are mainly xp, except in the art school.  i actually had java professor who was a devout mac user, he professed his love for macs in the first lecture, and got booed by most of the students

but i'm a political science major, and have been using linux for about 3 years now.  while the school does make distros available for download on their servers, linux is an after thought.  but the only problems i come across is that OO documents even though saved with microsoft compatibility don't open right in microsoft office.  i've had professors and TAs complain about line spacing and margins in my papers, and in one instance i was horrified to find that a presentation i made in OO was basically stripped of images and animations when i loaded it into powerpoint during a class presentation... i looked like an idiot standing in front of a black screen

### Post by pytheas22 on 2008-05-11
I attend an Ivy League school in upstate New York.  Despite a supposedly top-notch engineering program here and the billions and billions in the university's endowment, Linux gets no official support.  I know only a handful of other students who use it, and most of those are people I work with at the one computer lab on campus that runs dual-boot XP/Ubuntu--and after the end of this semester, we're getting rid of the Linux partition permanently because the full-time staff have deemed it a waste of resources.

Most of the kids in engineering, even in CS, don't seem to have much interest in Linux, as they're only interested in going on to big jobs at Microsoft (which does a lot of recruiting here) and friends.  We ran an installfest recently and despite a huge amount of advertising, only about a dozen people showed up.  My engineering friends also say it's hard to use Linux for coursework because professors require software that only runs on proprietary platforms.  I've used Linux exclusively for over a year, however, and have no problems, although I'm only a history major and don't need any special software.

I'm relieved to read about other schools that are Linux-friendly.  I guess that outside of the pretentious Ivy League, academia is still taking Linux seriously.

### Post by MaindotC on 2008-05-12
We offer a Unix/Linux class on campus, and I'd say 1/8th of the people that take it actually start to use it long-term.  It's not really supported at the help desk because if you use Linux you either figure out the problem yourself, or you know enough to research your own answers and understand it yourself instead of entrusting it to someone else without knowing the answer.

One of the big downers is we have access to a free DRM-enabled music service called Ruckus and I have yet been able to Wine it on our standard-issue Thinkpad T60's or 61's.  We have access to lab computers that we can install whatever we want whenever we want so I keep some applications like M$ SQLServer on a lab machine and just remote into it to do my db stuff there.

### Post by uraldinho on 2008-05-15
I was an engineering student once upon a time. We used windows. Looking back at it, I really don't understand why an engineering student uses windows. We learnt a lot about standards, etc, but there is no such thing as standards in windows, it goes against everything we were taught. All the components in the PC must pass very stringent government regulated tests, but windows doesn't care about IEEE standards and does not support standard APIs. 

Imagine a student who wants to do research on the implementation of TCP/IP. He/she has no chance of seeing windows code, and the only way of completing the project is to switch to a linux like system. So why not teach the students linux in the first place?

---

### Post by pytheas22 on 2008-05-15
> He/she has no chance of seeing windows code, and the only way of completing the project is to switch to a linux like system. So why not teach the students linux in the first place?

Well, in this case, the student could look at the BSD code, of course, since Microsoft lifted most of its TCP/IP stack from there.

### Post by M_Alex on 2008-05-15
I'm writing my PhD at Warsaw University (Poland), and I was very pleasently surprised to see that some libraries use Fedora based systems.

Unfortunatly, such cases are few and far between; a lot of people think that using an open source programme means using an unstable, unsecure and, sadly, low quality system. They have no idea that linux 

I recently brought my laptop to class in order to show the students i teach a few tricks in Open Office / MS office (my laptop dual boots). They were surprised that OOo can read / save *.doc format. Taking into account the financial situation of many students, open source software would be a nice choice - not just due to the prices of OS's, but the prices of computer equiptment - if the requirements get really to high you go XFCE (eg. xubuntu). Unfortunatly, many people think in the categories of something being free = low quality (at least here). They also are pretty afraid to experiment with OS's, as - to put it bluntly - a lot of the are computer illiterate. I still get an odd paper every once in a while, where I discover that the person writing it hit enter after every line, as s/he was unaware that the text wraps automatically.

### Post by pytheas22 on 2008-05-16

We have this problem too.  Microsoft "sponsors" us by giving free Vista laptops to professors, making deals with the library to index our books electronically and so on.  Adobe and other companies do similar things.  I don't understand how we're supposed to think freely when our academic agenda is perverted by corporations buying out faculty and departments.  I wish that Canonical or other more benign companies had the resources to make similar forays into academia so that there would at least be some resistance to the Microsoft monopoly here.

Also, Macs are popular too, but Apple is no better (actually in many respects worse) when it comes to software freedom and open source, or actually studying how a computer works (as opposed to learning to interact with its pretty, hardware-accelerated interface but not being allowed to see what's going on behind the scenes).

### Post by _DD_ on 2008-06-05
I'm 16. I'm just finishing secondary school (high school) and use linux all the time at home, but school is Windows based. They run an exchange server and use active directory so they're pretty tied to MS, although they've recently just kitted out two IT suites with new Macs which can work with MS active directory. 

They actually invited a friend and I to go and see if the permissions they'd set on the Macs didn't miss anything, seeing as we've been plaguing them with loopholes in their Windows setup for years :D

I don't think they'd be against kitting out an IT suite with linux, considering Novell have got active directory integration on Suse. We also convinced them yesterday to switch to a squid proxy rather than the rubbish and painfully slow windows filter server they run at the moment.

### Post by inchaos on 2008-06-05
I'm at a university with about 15,000 undergrads and our IT department recommends/supports windows and Mac OS.  I've been running Linux for the three years I've been in college and haven't ever been at the point where I needed windows for anything.

If I'm printing a paper for submission, the file extension doesn't matter and if I'm emailing/submitting, OO.org has great .doc and .xls support.  Our CS department uses EOS and Fedora after the first couple of classes which use Windows (but all the required software has Linux versions--yay!).  2/3 of our labs are windows and the other third are mac labs (mostly used by art students for whom macs are strongly recommended).  We have a few Linux labs used exclusively by CS students.

99% of the people in my CS classes use Windows exclusively and only use Linux because of the CS program.  I've only met a handful of people who use Linux exclusively and I feel like a total nerd even though you don't need to know a ton about computers to use Linux nowadays.  

I don't think I've ever seen a student in a common area with a Linux laptop other than myself.

That's better than the United States, where a lot of my classmates still have no idea what Linux is.

### Post by Mizzou_Engineer on 2008-06-19
> **SZF2001 said:**
> In this day and age, a lot of people are going to end up on one computer or another, I suppose. Rather it be a laptop or desktop, kids need to study.

Here is my question - are there a bunch of college kids running Linux, or is the general average Joe still going to be using Windows? No offense to them, of course, they are just sticking to what they know.

I was just curious about the subject - it would be neat to meet other people using Ubuntu or Mandrake, maybe Fedora, you know. I bet there are some classes I could take, too...

At my school, there were a few trends:

- The journalism department all use Apples and all the students did too because this was required for their classes.
- The biology department all use Apples so some of the students did, but probably less than half.
- The business department all uses Windows and AFAICT almost all of the students do too, maybe a random Apple user.
- The most popular OS in the engineering department was Windows but there were quite a few faculty and students running Linux, SunOS/Solaris or BSD. There were maybe a handful of Apple users.
- The physics department had the faculty on dual-boot Windows/Linux machines and the students used mostly Windows but a fair number used Linux at some point due to some programs being *nix-only.
- The CS department was also on dual-boot Windows/Linux machines and so were most of the students. There were both MS-centric and *nix-centric classes taught: MS for the intro/simple stuff like VB and *nix for Java, C, kernel/OS/compiler design. Very, very few people used Apple hardware and OSes and there were quite a few very vocal *nix-only users (who would avoid taking the MS-centric classes in favor of *nix-centric ones.)
- The rest of the university largely used Windows with about 1 in 10 people using an Apple. It is hard to gauge the number of *nix users as they don't all have a backlit penguin logo on their computers like the Apple users, but I have seen a *few* others out there. Usually they are running Ubuntu or Fedora, but there are a few running SuSE as well. 

The IT department supports Windows XP, Vista, and MacOS 10.4/10. and has basically a don't ask, don't tell policy regarding student use of other OSes. They will allow computers running Linux, BSD, Solaris, or PDA/phone OSes on the network but will not support it if something goes wrong. W2K and Win9x as well as MacOS 10.3 and earlier are banned from the network.

---

### Post by Mizzou_Engineer on 2008-06-19
> **SZF2001 said:**
> Eh, I kind of expected that. One thing I'm worried about is certain college's *expect* you to use MS, like getting onto servers and whatnot, things like encrypted .doc files... Is this true? I'd really hate having to dual boot again.

They "expect" you to run Windows but unless your classes have a specific software requirement (most do not) you just need to be able to do the following:

- Run a graphical Web browser (IE, Firefox and Safari are supported.)
- Use e-mail (POP3/IMAP4 or Webmail)
- Run a word processor and e-mail documents in a format somebody with a typical MS setup will be able to open read-only: .doc, RTF, PDF. 
- Run a spreadsheet app and be able to open .xls and .csv files and send as .xls files.
- Run a presentation program and be able to open and save .ppt files. 

That's it. Despite being told that the only acceptable software is either OS X + MS Office or Windows XP/Vista + MS Office, we have people running OS X + NeoOffice or iWork, Windows + OpenOffice or WordPerfect Office. There is another guy running Linux + OpenOffice and nobody is having any trouble with what they are running as the requirements are very general and almost any modern OS + office suite can handle them.

### Post by Zenze on 2008-06-19

### Post by BreakDecks on 2008-06-19
I go to the University of Memphis and our campus is pretty Linux-Friendly and I have met a few other Linux users myself.

Our school has a system they call iPrint.  It lets you use your computer to print to the nearest public printer and pay for it out of your private printing funds at $0.05/page (we get funds automatically that are included in our tuition, so I have over $200 in print credits, and I print a lot.  I doubt it is possible to run out, because they don't expire).

Anyway, the system uses your MAC address (that you register in a netreg system to be allowed to access the internet) to check your identity, and make logging into a print server unnecessary.  Anyway, they provide drivers for they system for Windows, Mac OSX and Linux over the campus intranet.

We have multiple UNIX labs on campus, and many of the graduate COMP students are required to use a Linux Distro, or Sun Solaris on their personal machines.  I have worked with a few of them to help them learn Linux (when I was a freshmen.  Then again, I have been using Linux for a few years, and some of them just started).

I run Ubuntu Linux.  I have a friend who is an Art Major who runs Ubuntu Studio.  I also have a few COMP friends who run Ubuntu.  (I am an MIS Major)  I work at the Campus ResNet Office (IT), and I have come by a few Fedora Core, Red Hat, Gentoo, and Ubuntu users.

Also, I have a co-worker that makes fun of me for always putting my slashes the wrong direction on the windows command prompt, calling me a UNIX addict.

I don't know about other campuses, but the University of Memphis seems very open to Linux and FOSS software.  We even have a chapter of the Association of Computing Machinery (ACM) that has hosted an Open-Source Software Symposium.

---

### Post by lian1238 on 2008-09-02

I think I've shared enough.:lolflag:

### Post by ender542 on 2008-09-04
Absolutely!

At least in my experience (3 years at the University of Rhode Island and one year in France), a lot of students are using it.  The majority are still primarily windows users, but a lot are interested in trying it (LiveCD or Dual Boot).  

There are still some programs required of some classes that require a Windows environment to run, but more and more classes are moving away from proprietary formats here at URI.  The network here has always supported every OS imaginable (Linux, Unix, Sun, Mac pre-OSX, Max OSX, DOS, and every version of Windows).  

The computer labs here are an equal mix of Mac and Windows, but the Engineering computer lab runs about 30 dual boot machines (Windows XP / Gentoo) and the Electrical Engineering, Computer Engineering, Biomedical Engineering, and Computer Science Departments all run and require at least some Linux/Unix use.

The campus servers are a mix of Linux, Unix, and Windows machines depending on their purpose.  I actually (still a student, year 5 of 5) am setting up my computer to be triple-boot (Windows XP, Ubuntu, and a trial OS).  I admit that I am on the fringe of the range, but I am not lonely out there.  (I also must mention that I am a Chemical Engineering major, not Computer Science, Computer Engineering, or Electrical Engineering)

---

### Post by brawd on 2008-11-18
Having taught at colleges as well as other work around colleges, mainly in the engineering departments I can tell you that most teachers can barely read their email, let alone compose documents or data tables using any sort of OS. Notice how many of them have hand drawn overheads because they can't use any programs to create anything. Most colleges have an intranet of some sort, or a version of moodle, that lies mainly disused on the servers. My own site is constantly raided to bulk up the department site with entries.

Try submitting an electronic file for an assignment to a teacher to cut down on paper. It won't be allowed. The authorities that govern schools won't allow that either. They reckon that doesn't show 'provable' learning and hence teachers don't have to know about computers.

Few people in the UK understand the paucity of computer knowledge that exists in college teaching staff. On the other hand the support workers that keep the colleges open are usually mustard - they can find a game to play anywhere they can log on.

Until the teachers gain a bit of competence on any sort of computer they are going to be a millstone around the students neck.
Linux in colleges, not much chance I'm afraid.

regards,

brawd.

### Post by pytheas22 on 2008-11-18
> ive read up on gtkpod. but i cant find the install instructions. is anyone willing to help out?

Very easy.  Just open a terminal and type:
```

sudo apt-get install gtkpod
```

Alternatively, if you prefer to install it via a graphical interface, go to Applications>Add/Remove and search for 'gtkpod'.  Either way, it will download and install gtkpod for you; I think you can then find it under the Applications>Sound and Video menu.

If this doesn't work, however, you should probably start a new thread so that this one doesn't get off-topic.

---

### Post by rsambuca on 2008-11-18
You probably want to install gtkpod-aac.  This is the gtkpod version with aac/mp4 support.  It is in the multiverse repositories, so if you just open your Synaptic Package Manager (System - Administration - Synaptic), you can install it by first searching for it, and then selecting "install".  Simple as that!

---

### Post by SpaceMaster on 2008-11-18
As an answer to the initial question of this thread:
Yes, but not a statistically significant number.
Evidence: I am a college student using Linux.  In fact, it's been nearly two years since I switched over.  However, I am one of few.  The authentication server for access to the campus network logs the operating system of all logged-on machines.  The proportions of Linux users on this campus have steadily hovered around the 1% mark of total users.  It briefly reached 5% during the launch of Vista, when people refused to upgrade to it.  However, many of these students have since sold their old PCs in favor of Macs.

I use linux 100% on my personal computer. I carry around a wacom graphire 4 for taking notes (like in calculus) with my gateway laptop that's running ubuntu.

I'm a CS major, and I know that they do have some ubuntu boxes in one of the CS labs, but I haven't made it that far in my edumakation, so I'm not really sure what they're used for.

They teach c++ in CS I and II, and use visual studio for that. I, however, don't touch it, instead preferring to use vi & g++ (with a nice little shell script I called 'compile_and_run_with_g++', which works well from :! within vi).

So far I haven't had a single class that I've /had/ to have something running windows. I'm not 100% sure about our assembly class/data structures next semester, but I guess I'll find out :D

The kid behind me in CS II just got a laptop and now has it dual booting between windows and ubuntu. This other kid who's completely tech-illiterate in my econ class was looking at installing it, but his computer doesn't have enough leftover space from all his windows stuff.

Other than that, there aren't too many people here who use linux (although I installed ubuntu on some "public" access terminals we have... they used to have windows, with unimpeded admin access on them. You can imagine how much crap was stuck on there. After about 30 seconds of waiting for the start menu to come up I decided they'd get ubuntu. I set them up to be auto-logged in to a basically privalege-less user. I must confess great mirth when I check on my "babies" and find that someone has tried to "install" 'kewlGamezXXXtr0janz0r.txt.exe' or something to that effect... mmmm, silly folksies, viruses are for Windows! So technically every one of those folks use linux, they probably just don't notice/care:lolflag:)

---

### Post by fredbird67 on 2008-11-23
At the liberal-arts college I work for, all our I.T. guys know is Windows and could quite frankly care less about Linux. I'd like to see 'em at least use it on the server end if nothing else, but they don't even do that.  Not to mention, we're kinda cash-strapped as it is, yet they still don't give a rat's rear end about learning another operating system.  Stupid...

Naturally, I see a lot of students use Windows laptops, although I do see the occasional Macintosh laptop here and there every so often.  I work as a reference librarian, plus I'm also the library's unofficial computer nerd.  I've brought in a few live Linux CD's every once in a while, because I do need them sometimes.  For instance, at least a few times a week, students will have problems getting Windows to recognize their flash drives and need something off of there to print.  I first try it on my Windows box at my desk, and if the flash drive doesn't show up on it, then I pop in a Puppy Linux CD -- and Linux has recognized each and every flash drive every time! :)

Yesterday (Friday) morning, I had a student that needed a file off of his laptop, which was running Windows, but his laptop wouldn't boot up.  No problem, Linux to the rescue!  And there, I was able to retrieve that file, save it to my flash drive, plug my flash drive in to my Windows box at my desk, print it out for him, and I had a very satisfied patron! :)

Fred in St. Louis, MO USA

### Post by pytheas22 on 2008-11-23

I have to agree with the poster above that Cornell tech support leaves a lot to be desired, especially in the Linux department.  In my experience (I graduated last spring), as soon as I mention that I'm using Linux, they tell me that they can't provide any further support--even if the software I'm running is in a Windows XP virtual machine and thus really has nothing to do with Linux.

### Post by jamesrl on 2008-12-07
I think that expecting a university to provide tech support for your own computer is a little much, especially if you are using one of the thousands of distros of linux.  I'm at cornell right now, and almost everyone I know uses linux or Macs, but doesn't expect tech support.  [E.g., if you have to connect to the cornell network, you can do it following OS-free instructions; or if you have to register for classes you can do it from the web based tools, etc.]  

Unlike an earlier Cornell poster, I think Macs are a decent OS for people who want to pay for a *nix operating environment with a stable set of programs developed to work on your particular hardware setup.  The trendy ads and the associated pricetags prevent me from getting one, but there's been many times where I had some smallish problem make me almost wish I knew my hardware was fully supported.  [E.g., some problems waking up from suspend on my laptop -- it works 90% of the time, but then sometimes the screen won't start up.  Or in the past wifi issues where I had to use ndiswrapper and recompile it everytime the kernel changed.]  Mac users typically don't deal with stuff that.  Then again I really like the open-source nature of linux, and having the command line be the default method for doing many things that don't need to be GUI (and shouldn't be).  I also dislike how Macs do not have middle/right-click capabilities, and you really can't navigate the OS by keyboard or mouse alone (which is really frustrating when one isn't available).  I find it much easier to install things in linux (debian package manager beats fink and anything similar I've seen for macs) and the gnome-terminal with ubuntu defaults is much better than the terminal that comes with OS X (resizing windows after they are opened, actually works properly, ls has color, etc).

My departments (physics) computer lab is all ubuntu machines (earlier they were running debian) as they did found out going open-source and getting new hardware and paying a student to manage it was cheaper than licensing fees for costly OS and the proprietary software (e.g., Matlab, mathematica, etc.).   Sure matlab is a bit nicer than octave, but it isn't worth the cost to maintain on every computer (e.g., if you do research for someone they might pay for it, but otherwise you use the free octave).

---

### Post by UanarchyK on 2008-12-07

### Post by jenkinbr on 2008-12-18
Short version:
Yes, I've been running Ubuntu for quite a while now, on an Offline installation.  Works great for everything I need it for, and lately, have had no problems installing new software (see below).

#########> Long Version <#########

I've been running Ubuntu for some time now while going to school.  I had talked to the IT guy at the community college in Northern Minnesota that I attend and told him that I had an older (Pentium 4) computer running windows, but couldn't afford software.  I had also told him that I would eventually like to start doing some web development on the computer.  He was going to simply recommended I used OO.o with Windows, until I mentioned web development, at which point he began to list several Linux distros that would be easier to use for web server and web scripting tasks.  The systems he suggested were OpenSUSE and CentOS.  I then told him that I had tried several distributions of Linux in the past, and had some trouble figuring out how to use them.  To this, he told me about Ubuntu being very easy for a new Linux user, and gave me an Edubuntu 7.04 disc to try.

I was barely able to get the live CD running on my computer (only 512MB of RAM), but was able to install to a spare hard drive I had using the manual partitioning.  Once the install was complete, I booted into it, and felt it a little cheesy, so I went and downloaded the Ubuntu 7.04 Alternate CD, which ran better.

I liked plain Ubuntu better than Edubuntu, up until I found out about software dependencies.  I was rather disappointed, because I run (even to this day) an entirely internet-disconnected computer because we can't afford it.  This caused me to put Ubuntu aside for a while, while I continued to use Windows XP with OpneOffice, but I still liked the feel of Ubuntu, and tried again in November of 2007, with a freshly downloaded copy of 7.04.(I had lost the original, and downloaded a new copy only a day before the release of 7.10, unknown to me at the time..)  I figured this time, I would be stubborn and try to track down all the dependencies that I needed to run Ubuntu.

After chasing through the dependency tree, with all of it's looping branches and such, I decided to give up on installing software, but began to use the system more and more.  I read some of the system-provided documentation, learned about the 6 month release cycle, and also about the way Ubuntu names releases.  It was then that I realized that there should be a new version out, and downloaded 7.10 in December 2007, and had problems with my graphics card, so I posted here on these forums to find out what I could do about it.

Then, I decided to browse around the forums some more, did a few searches, and stumbled upon [this post]("http://ubuntuforums.org/showthread.php?t=572819").  Finally!  A way for me to install software to an offline computer!  This worked great, as long as I stayed away from packages that made other downloads as part of the install script (msttcorefonts and flashplugin-nonfree, I'm looking at you...) and packages that depend upon those packages (ubuntu-restricted-extras).  Overall, this worked great.

Installed 8.04, this time with a separate /home partition shortly ofter it's release, and began to use Ubuntu exclusively.  Unfortunately, NoNetDebs went down one day, and never came back.  My PM to epimeteo still remains unanswered to this day.  Several alternatives to NoNetDebs were offered, but I could not get any of them to work, due to the way the computers here on campus are configured.  So, I was forced to do more research, and after many, many twists on the search terms I fed to google, I came across [this Ubuntu Wiki page]("https://help.ubuntu.com/community/AptGet/Offline/Repository"), which described how to create a removable repository on a USB flash drive, and gave it a try.  It worked! (I now have this method working great by using the WinWGet Download Manager)  The download script generated by synaptic works wonders (after modification)  I do hope to post a tutorial regarding this relatively soon, but finals currently occupy a good portion of my time.

Linux support on campus is minimal, but is present. IT currently runs SUSE on the IT computers, but maintains Windows machines for staff and student use.  IT does encourage the use of FOSS so I am currently typing this at school using Firefox.

At home, I still have windows on a separate partition, but I have resized it down to about 12GB, because the only thing I use windows for is to scan, and I rarely need to do that anymore.

---

### Post by Twilight in Zero on 2008-12-19
I'm a Computer Science student at Northern Kentucky University. I actually have two friends there who run Ubuntu on their laptops. Unfortunately, there's no real support for Linux; the IT site's instructions includes directions for only Windows and Mac OS. But I've been able to get by just fine.

I see a lot of Macbooks popping up on campus as of late, and it's a breath of fresh air to know that at least three people on campus use Linux. I've talked to a few of the Mac users, and they get Macs because they're tired of Windows. It baffles me that they do that when they could try Linux before jumping to spend OVER 9000 dollars on an overpriced computer just to get away from Windows. Of course, I understand that not everyone's experience with Linux would be as good as mine, that's why I say "try".

As for the other Mac users, they switch because--and this ticks me off a bit--apparently, Macs are **required** for art majors. That makes no sense.

I've talked to a few of the Mac users, and they get Macs because they're tired of Windows. It baffles me that they do that when they could try Linux before jumping to spend OVER 9000 dollars on an overpriced computer just to get away from Windows. Of course, I understand that not everyone's experience with Linux would be as good as mine, that's why I say "try".

As for the other Mac users, they switch because--and this ticks me off a bit--apparently, Macs are **required** for art majors. That makes no sense.

Wow, I just clicked on the "last page" portion of this thread while I was perusing the forum and wouldn't you know... another person who goes to Northern Kentucky University. You can increment your linux user count at NKU to four. I've had it on my Dell Vostro for over one and a half years now.

Small world isn't it?

Are you a CIT/CSC major? I would expect that, seeing as how you're interested in linux and open source.

### Post by Defrector on 2008-12-21
MTU, an engineering school, makes everyone use solaris thin workstations for engineering and math coursework. The CS department is very deep into linux/solaris, almost exclusively. A lot of students have a linux box as a hobby machine and a considerable number use it as their primary system.

Some students hate having to use *nix like OSes, usually because it involves re-learning from their windows background. Some don't want to ever see it again, others fall in love with it. The CS majors slant toward falling in love with it, probably because its open nature takes feature constraints off the OS. I see CS majors who are into macs as well.

Likewise, the arts/media/audio production majors tend to lean toward macs but some are dabbling in *nix and windows.

Most business majors I've seen use windows almost exclusively.

### Post by pytheas22 on 2008-12-24

OO 3.0 supports .docx out-of-the-box.  There are plenty of guides on how to upgrade to 3.0 if you google.

Alternatively, you can easily add .docx support to OO 2.x; see [this page]("http://librarycomputerguy.wordpress.com/2007/10/20/open-ms-word-2007-docx-documents-in-ubuntu/").

### Post by halucinations on 2009-01-03
I am at the University of Kansas and I have switched to Ubuntu.  I also have TinyXP w/ GeoShell working through a virtual machine (takes about 1 minute to boot up!) just in case I need windows for a class.  Also, I have office 2007 installed on the virtual machine.  

I have used Ubuntu in the past but I always had to go back to Windows for some odd reason.  But with this virtual machine I don't foresee any of those reasons making me switch back.  I really like Ubuntu, currently I am on Intrepid, because most of all I don't have to deal with viruses and spyware, and I really like the amount of applications that are available to choose from.  The multiple desktops are very handy as well, and compiz is always fun.

I have not seen or heard of anyone else using Linux though...

### Post by jwkolberg on 2009-01-05
I think 5 years ago it wouldn't of mattered what OS you ran becuase you just printed out papers or did research on a computer. Now, many of my proffesors require papers to be turned in online and under no circumstances can ever be anything but .doc

Eventhough openoffice and others have support for .doc I have ran into issues where the proffessor grades the paper using Word and leaves comments that can't be read when using openoffice.  

Also many profs use text books that come with an online testing center, where tests on done online and a microsoft only application has to be installed and can only be ran on IE, leaving mac users out as well.

My college's CS ans IT (UNM) departments have fully embraced linux including more than one computer center with Ubuntu work stations.

### Post by gnomeuser on 2009-01-05
I recently had a college experience and the school network was administered on Linux (RHEL5, used to be Fedora). Everywhere you had the choice of sitting down with a Windows XP machine or a RHEL terminal, often Mac would also be offered. So the university adapted to your choices which was very nice and I must say I enjoyed being able to sit down and work with RHEL as I have years of experience using Red Hat Linux and Fedora so I feel comfortable in that environment.

As for the students themselves, I would wager out of the 40 or so people in my CS class roughly half were on Linux. Ubuntu was the most popular distro in my casual survey. 

Every assignment we were given could be completed on Linux, programming classes was in Java (using BlueJ and Greenfoot both proprietary closed source applications). A few classes required Windows due to application demands, but you were not required to install it, the school offered access to plenty machines as well as workspace.

So yes, college students do indeed use Linux and many of them are also Linux developers, e.g. in my study group I was fortunate enough to meet a very nice Foresight Linux developer (ermo).

### Post by Maheriano on 2009-01-05
In Canada we have both colleges and universities but university is our equivalent of your college. I went to Acadia University out east and they have something called an Acadia Advantage where every student gets a laptop at no extra cost (it's already the most expensive undergraduate school in the country). Students studying computer science such as myself had to take said laptop at the beginning of every year and take it down to the help centre and have then dual boot it with Linux because we needed it to compile C code for courses. You're free to use whichever compiler you want but I knew nothing about C nor Linux at the time so I just used whatever everyone else used....Linux. At first (1999 - ~2001) it was just a command line compiler and we were working just with standard output. Somewhere around 2002 it looked like what I now recognize as a KDE environment so I can only assume it was Ubuntu on the backend, not sure. But somewhere in there, there is a free C compiler on the command line and the univeristy was too cheap to buy licenses so that's what I learned to code on. Other than that I didn't use Linux for anything, I got frustrated with it after it wouldn't work for me like Windows does and would simply boot into Windows to make my life easier at the time.

### Post by Varyan's Faith on 2009-01-15
I'm a student at UNF and have met only a limited number of people who've had any direct experience with linux.  I personally use Ubuntu on my laptop as a dual boot with XP. I only keep my windows partition around because I work daily with Adobe Premiere Pro which is finicky when it runs under wine.  But for all intents and purposes, I primarily use linux for everything. 

I've installed linux on my parents computer and my sister's laptop. My sister's laptop was acting erratically and half the time wouldn't boot right. After loading Ubuntu, it ran better than new.

I work as tech support for the business college and I have been building up a   Linux library for us to test out software on in order to support linux.  We also currently use linux servers and we are about to upload another private linux server for the tech departent.  

Linux is around at UNF, but mainly just among the computer science majors and engineering majors.  Hopefully that will change with time, especially since the university stopped supporting macs.
