### Post by Belerophon on 2009-02-19
I'm from Portugal but still I'm compelled to give some feedback about that matter.

I'm studying at Oporto, taking a course on informatics at the Engineering Faculty (FEUP - Faculdade de Engenharia da Universidade do Porto).

I'm glad to say that Linux (since a few years ago, Ubuntu) is a part of our lives there. For example, the Operating Systems class is totally dedicated to Linux (in part, due to the nature of the operating system). The computer rooms for the students dual boot with ubuntu and Windows. In fact, all computers that I know on the faculty either have only Linux or Linux and Windows. Through most of our lives in my course, we give a big importance to interoperability of our projects, to make them compile and run under Linux and Windows (at least). At other level, the department relies upon Linux for the management of the network. Finally, I can say that at least 30% of the students in our course use Linux actively.

Windows, of course, still plays a big important role there. And, in my opinion, it will continue to play. Nor I think it should be other way. We are students, and we should learn to adapt to the circumstances: there are N operating systems, than let's try (the best we can) to make products that work on those N operating systems. And even better, be capable of identifying the advantages of using one or other operating system in a given case.

I know that I didn't follow the course of the discussion, but I thought it was important to say this.
(PS: excuse my poor english)

Thank you.

### Post by Peter Frank on 2009-02-20
I study physics at a University in England. The uni has a lot of *nix background. All the PCs in the computer rooms dual boot OpenSuSE with Windows. The C++ programming lesson in the physics department is taught using Linux. The lecturers typically have used *nix since the 1980s and have no Windows programming experience at all. G++ and Gnuplot are the main weapons of our computing class, so it's very FOSS friendly.

It turns out that nearly all students use Linux in the college computer rooms solely for the purpose of doing the class excercises. Most are not aware that Linux could be installed on their own PCs, and even fewer realize they could do those C++ excercises on Windows as well...

### Post by 123_854 on 2009-03-27

Actually, I put Ubuntu Studio 8.10 on my laptop and the multimedia apps don't run so well (I have the latest drivers for my hardware). I rely on Vista more often for this reason.

### Post by Nickedynick on 2010-03-11
I'm in my final (4th) year at Cardiff University, and I only really discovered Linux after starting here. Since I discovered it, I've been playing around with various distros but I always seem to come back to Ubuntu.

However, because of the nature of my course (Mechanical Engineering), I have to have a copy of Windows available. Things like AutoCAD, SolidWorks, etc. just don't play well in Wine, as much as I'd like them to. I always go into Ubuntu to do general work though - it's just a nicer, quicker environment to work in.

The other thing keeping Windows on my system is games. I'm a pretty big PC gamer (half down to being too poor to buy a console), and until the market starts developing for Linux I'll have a copy of MS's "finest" on my system. Hopefully that won't be too long - I'm praying that Valve's porting of Steam to Mac will be a signal to other publishers to seriously consider other OSs. But we'll see!

---

### Post by sexybeast099 on 2010-05-01
To answer your question, like the rest of the posters, I am a college student and I use linux.  Specifically Ubuntu is my flavor of choice and I've toyed with it since mid-high school.  My University is not Ivy League and is widely considered a "teacher's college" so only MS Windows and Mac OSX are supported for the most part.  Having computer classes (not exclusive to Linux) introduces the idea of better security, less space, etc. for Linux, but they require MS proprietary software specifically.

EDIT: Since Mac and MS are split down the middle with users at my college, the university tries to make most applications platform-independent. i.e. programs are typically in Java or web-based.

It seemed as if you were asking if Joe Bluecollar uses Linux or if it's all people who have a college education, etc... etc...

To answer *that*, I don't see Ubuntu (supposedly the most user-friendly flavor of Linux) at that point in the development.  I don't think it ever will be either if it is to remain free.  MS and Mac sell their products for money, thereby increasing their income to pour back into development and tech support.  I dare say all forms of Linux are free and will not have as many resources to provide the support the general populous is used to.  Grandma Jenkins may have no clue what a forum is, but she sure as hell knows how to call a tech support line and can follow a "Click here, then here. Type this word, click apply, then close".

---

### Post by SeijiSensei on 2011-10-04
My daughter would be using her Kubuntu installation if her campus supported Linux connections to its proprietary wifi network.  They only support Windows and OS X.  Worse, they discontinued their wired network so she can't even plug an Ethernet cable into the wall socket in her dorm room.

When we visited campuses a couple years' back, I asked about Linux support.  Most of these were smaller liberal-arts colleges; Linux was largely viewed as the product of alien life-forms.  (Perhaps it is!) I wrote about this experience at [Slashdot]("http://linux.slashdot.org/story/09/09/08/1729258/does-your-college-or-university-support-linux") which resulted in a lot of commentary. Here's a  [more recent thread](http://ask.slashdot.org/story/11/06/10/136234/ask-slashdot-linux-support-in-universities) on the same topic.

### Post by layers on 2012-01-20
First of all, when I go to the library, everyone has a Mac - 99% of students.

Anyways, in the Engineering dept., roughly about 400 out of the total 800 computer systems are Red Hat. And keep in mind, that those 400 PC's are the ones with simulation software.

I've been using Ubuntu since 1st year, in 2nd I completely wiped Windows 7, after the latest "security update". I still need to use VBox with XP on it for certain programs though (Eclipse, Spice). Nice at least MatLab's here...

And the CompSci dept. has another 200 machines with some KDE on them. Also, the most powerfull computer on campus (and one of the most powerful in the world for that matter) is running a custom Linux.

So, in short, yes.

### Post by Welly Wu on 2012-02-12
I plan to pursue my Masters of Arts in English Writing Studies at Montclair State University in Upper Montclair, New Jersey USA. They all use Microsoft Windows 7 64 bit and a handful of people use Apple MacBook Pros or Airs. Microsoft Office is the default office productivity suite. I expect to be the only one using Ubuntu 12.10 64 bit this fall 2012 semester when I begin graduate school. I already earned my Bachelor of Arts in English Creative Writing from Montclair State University so I know the system and the community pretty well. Nothing has changed since I graduated in June 2004. There are more buildings and more degrees being offered, but everybody there still uses Microsoft Windows 7 64 bit or Apple Macintosh OS X on their MacBook Pros. It is a very affluent school filled with a bunch of wealthy kids and well paid tenured professors on staff.

### Post by Tac2 on 2012-02-13

The print quality of that paper is going to kick ***. I am in love with LaTeX and tikz is just plain awesome.

In the engineering building at my University they mostly have Linux Computers, I believe Red Hat, not bad considering that the University is the home to Linux Kernel and Linus Torvalds sits on the board of directors.

I did start to see more pad/tablet computers by students and a few professors. Mostly iPads and Amazon's Kindle fire. The kindle fire was big christmas gift item given to college students, again a girl amazed me as she rooted her kindle fire right away with a little help via Google.
